</div><div class="mw-content-ltr mw-parser-output" lang="en" dir="ltr"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Process of forming order by local interactions</div>

<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Nb3O7(OH)_self-organization2.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Nb3O7%28OH%29_self-organization2.jpg/330px-Nb3O7%28OH%29_self-organization2.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="174" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Nb3O7%28OH%29_self-organization2.jpg/500px-Nb3O7%28OH%29_self-organization2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Nb3O7%28OH%29_self-organization2.jpg/960px-Nb3O7%28OH%29_self-organization2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2013" data-file-height="1170" /></a><figcaption>Self-organization in micron-sized Nb<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>(OH) cubes during a <a href="/wiki/Hydrothermal_synthesis" title="Hydrothermal synthesis">hydrothermal treatment</a> at 200 °C. <div class="sidebar-list mw-collapsible mw-collapsed"><div class="sidebar-list-title" style="background:transparent;border-top:1px solid #aaa;text-align:center;;color: var(--color-base)"><a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">Evolution</a> and <a href="/wiki/Adaptation" title="Adaptation">adaptation</a></div><div class="sidebar-list-content mw-collapsible-content"><a href="/wiki/Neural_network_(machine_learning)" title="Neural network (machine learning)">Artificial neural network</a><br />

</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-content">
<div class="sidebar-list mw-collapsible mw-collapsed"><div class="sidebar-list-title" style="background:transparent;border-top:1px solid #aaa;text-align:center;;color: var(--color-base)"><a href="/wiki/Game_theory" title="Game theory">Game theory</a></div><div class="sidebar-list-content mw-collapsible-content"><a href="/wiki/Prisoner%27s_dilemma" title="Prisoner&#39;s dilemma">Prisoner's dilemma</a><br />
<p><a href="/wiki/Rational_choice_model" title="Rational choice model">Rational choice theory</a><br />
<a href="/wiki/Bounded_rationality" title="Bounded rationality">Bounded rationality</a><br />
</p>
<a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_game_theory" title="Evolutionary game theory">Evolutionary game theory</a><br /></div></div></td>
</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333133064" /><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1239400231">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}html.skin-theme-clientpref-night .mw-parser-output .navbar li a abbr{color:var(--color-base)!important}@media(prefers-color-scheme:dark){html.skin-theme-clientpref-os .mw-parser-output .navbar li a abbr{color:var(--color-base)!important}}@media print{.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:none!important}}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Complex_systems" title="Template:Complex systems"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Complex_systems" title="Template talk:Complex systems"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a href="/wiki/Special:EditPage/Template:Complex_systems" title="Special:EditPage/Template:Complex systems"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Self-organization</b>, also called <a href="/wiki/Spontaneous_order" title="Spontaneous order">spontaneous order</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Social_science" title="Social science">social sciences</a>, is a process where some form of overall <a href="/wiki/Order_and_disorder" title="Order and disorder">order</a> arises from local interactions between parts of an initially disordered <a href="/wiki/System" title="System">system</a>. The process can be spontaneous when sufficient energy is available, not needing control by any external agent. It is often triggered by seemingly random <a href="/wiki/Statistical_fluctuations" title="Statistical fluctuations">fluctuations</a>, amplified by <a href="/wiki/Positive_feedback" title="Positive feedback">positive feedback</a>. The resulting organization is wholly decentralized, <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/distribute" class="extiw" title="wikt:distribute">distributed</a> over all the components of the system. As such, the organization is typically <a href="/wiki/Robustness" title="Robustness">robust</a> and able to survive or <a href="/wiki/Self-healing_material" title="Self-healing material">self-repair</a> substantial <a href="/wiki/Perturbation_theory" title="Perturbation theory">perturbation</a>. <a href="/wiki/Chaos_theory" title="Chaos theory">Chaos theory</a> discusses self-organization in terms of islands of <a href="/wiki/Predictability" title="Predictability">predictability</a> in a sea of chaotic unpredictability.
</p><p>Self-organization is a phenomenon that occurs across various fields, including <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">physics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chemistry" title="Chemistry">chemistry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">biology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Collective_behavior" title="Collective behavior">collective behavior</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ecology" title="Ecology">ecology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Communication_networks" class="mw-redirect" title="Communication networks">communication networks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Robotics" title="Robotics">robotics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Artificial_intelligence" title="Artificial intelligence">artificial intelligence</a>, <a href="/wiki/Linguistics" title="Linguistics">linguistics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Social_science" title="Social science">social science</a>, <a href="/wiki/Urbanism" title="Urbanism">urbanism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Engineering" title="Engineering">engineering</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Examples of self-organization include <a href="/wiki/Crystallization" title="Crystallization">crystallization</a>, thermal <a href="/wiki/Convection" title="Convection">convection</a> of fluids, <a href="/wiki/Chemical_oscillator" title="Chemical oscillator">chemical oscillation</a>, animal <a href="/wiki/Swarming" class="mw-redirect" title="Swarming">swarming</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neural_circuit" title="Neural circuit">neural circuits</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Black_market" title="Black market">black markets</a>.
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Overview">Overview</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Self-organization&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Overview"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Self-organization is realized<sup id="cite&#95;ref-G&amp;P&#95;1971&#95;3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-G&amp;P_1971-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> in the <a href="/wiki/Extremal_principles_in_non-equilibrium_thermodynamics" title="Extremal principles in non-equilibrium thermodynamics">physics of non-equilibrium processes</a>, and in <a href="/wiki/Chemical_reaction" title="Chemical reaction">chemical reactions</a>, where it is often characterized as <a href="/wiki/Self-assembly" title="Self-assembly">self-assembly</a>. The concept has proven useful in biology, from the molecular to the <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">ecosystem</a> level.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Cited examples of self-organizing behavior also appear in the literature of many other disciplines, both in the <a href="/wiki/Natural_sciences" class="mw-redirect" title="Natural sciences">natural sciences</a> and in the <a href="/wiki/Social_sciences" class="mw-redirect" title="Social sciences">social sciences</a> (such as <a href="/wiki/Economics" title="Economics">economics</a> or <a href="/wiki/Anthropology" title="Anthropology">anthropology</a>). Self-organization has also been observed in mathematical systems such as  <a href="/wiki/Cellular_automaton" title="Cellular automaton">cellular automata</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:1&#95;5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Self-organization is an example of the related concept of <a href="/wiki/Emergence" title="Emergence">emergence</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Self-organization relies on four basic ingredients:<sup id="cite&#95;ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<ol><li>strong dynamical non-linearity, often (though not necessarily) involving  <a href="/wiki/Positive_feedback" title="Positive feedback">positive</a> and <a href="/wiki/Negative_feedback" title="Negative feedback">negative feedback</a></li>
<li>balance of exploitation and exploration</li>
<li>multiple interactions among components</li>
<li>availability of energy (to overcome the natural tendency toward <a href="/wiki/Entropy" title="Entropy">entropy</a>, or loss of free energy)</li></ol>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Principles">Principles</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Self-organization&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Principles"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The cybernetician <a href="/wiki/William_Ross_Ashby" class="mw-redirect" title="William Ross Ashby">William Ross Ashby</a> formulated the original principle of self-organization in 1947.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ashby1947&#95;8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ashby1947-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It states that any deterministic <a href="/wiki/Dynamic_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Dynamic system">dynamic system</a> automatically evolves towards a state of equilibrium that can be described in terms of an <a href="/wiki/Attractor" title="Attractor">attractor</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Attractor" title="Attractor">basin</a> of surrounding states. Once there, the further evolution of the system is constrained to remain in the attractor. This constraint implies a form of mutual dependency or coordination between its constituent components or subsystems. In Ashby's terms, each subsystem has adapted to the environment formed by all other subsystems.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ashby1947&#95;8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ashby1947-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The cybernetician <a href="/wiki/Heinz_von_Foerster" title="Heinz von Foerster">Heinz von Foerster</a> formulated the principle of "<a href="/wiki/Order_and_disorder" title="Order and disorder">order</a> from <a href="/wiki/Noise_(signal_processing)" title="Noise (signal processing)">noise</a>" in 1960.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It notes that self-organization is facilitated by random perturbations ("noise") that let the system explore a variety of states in its state space. This increases the chance that the system will arrive into the basin of a "strong" or "deep" attractor, from which it then quickly enters the attractor itself. The biophysicist <a href="/wiki/Henri_Atlan" title="Henri Atlan">Henri Atlan</a> developed this concept by proposing the principle of "<a href="/wiki/Complexity" title="Complexity">complexity</a> from noise"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> (<a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>: <i lang="fr">le principe de complexité par le bruit</i>)<sup id="cite&#95;ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> first in the 1972 book <i>L'organisation biologique et la théorie de l'information</i> and then in the 1979 book <i>Entre le cristal et la fumée</i>. The physicist and chemist <a href="/wiki/Ilya_Prigogine" title="Ilya Prigogine">Ilya Prigogine</a> formulated a similar principle as "order through fluctuations"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> or "order out of chaos".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It is applied in the method of <a href="/wiki/Simulated_annealing" title="Simulated annealing">simulated annealing</a> for <a href="/wiki/Problem_solving" title="Problem solving">problem solving</a> and <a href="/wiki/Machine_learning" title="Machine learning">machine learning</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><span class="anchor" id="Human&#95;society"></span>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="History">History</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Self-organization&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: History"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1320445320">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}@media print{body.ns-0 .mw-parser-output .hatnote{display:none!important}}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Spontaneous_order" title="Spontaneous order">Spontaneous order</a></div>
<p>The idea that the <a href="/wiki/Dynamics_(mechanics)" title="Dynamics (mechanics)">dynamics</a> of a system can lead to an increase in its organization has a long history. The ancient <a href="/wiki/Atomism" title="Atomism">atomists</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Democritus" title="Democritus">Democritus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lucretius" title="Lucretius">Lucretius</a> believed that a designing intelligence is unnecessary to create order in nature, arguing that given enough time and space and matter, order emerges by itself.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The philosopher <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Descartes" title="René Descartes">René Descartes</a> presents self-organization hypothetically in the fifth part of his 1637 <i><a href="/wiki/Discourse_on_Method" class="mw-redirect" title="Discourse on Method">Discourse on Method</a></i>. He elaborated on the idea in his unpublished work <i><a href="/wiki/The_World_(Descartes)" class="mw-redirect" title="The World (Descartes)">The World</a></i>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>a<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Immanuel_Kant" title="Immanuel Kant">Immanuel Kant</a> used the term "self-organizing" in his 1790 <i><a href="/wiki/Critique_of_Judgment" title="Critique of Judgment">Critique of Judgment</a></i>, where he argued that <a href="/wiki/Teleology" title="Teleology">teleology</a> is a meaningful concept only if there exists such an entity whose parts or "organs" are simultaneously ends and means. Such a system of organs must be able to behave as if it has a mind of its own, that is, it is capable of governing itself.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Kant&#95;19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kant-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1244412712">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 32px}.mw-parser-output .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;margin-top:0}@media(min-width:500px){.mw-parser-output .templatequotecite{padding-left:1.6em}}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>In such a natural product as this every part is thought as <i>owing</i> its presence to the agency of all the remaining parts, and also as existing <i>for the sake of the others</i> and of the whole, that is as an instrument, or organ... The part must be an organ <i>producing</i> the other parts—each, consequently, reciprocally producing the others... Only under these conditions and upon these terms can such a product be an <i>organized</i> and <i>self-organized</i> being, and, as such, be called a <i>physical end</i>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Kant&#95;19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kant-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p><a href="/wiki/Nicolas_L%C3%A9onard_Sadi_Carnot" title="Nicolas Léonard Sadi Carnot">Sadi Carnot</a> (1796–1832) and <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_Clausius" title="Rudolf Clausius">Rudolf Clausius</a> (1822–1888) discovered the <a href="/wiki/Second_law_of_thermodynamics" title="Second law of thermodynamics">second law of thermodynamics</a> in the 19th century. It states that total <a href="/wiki/Entropy" title="Entropy">entropy</a>, sometimes understood as disorder, will always increase over time in an <a href="/wiki/Isolated_system" title="Isolated system">isolated system</a>. This means that a system cannot spontaneously increase its order without an external relationship that decreases order elsewhere in the system (e.g. through consuming the low-entropy energy of a battery and diffusing high-entropy heat).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>18th-century thinkers had sought to understand the "universal laws of form" to explain the observed forms of living organisms. This idea became associated with <a href="/wiki/Lamarckism" title="Lamarckism">Lamarckism</a> and fell into disrepute until the early 20th century, when <a href="/wiki/D%27Arcy_Wentworth_Thompson" title="D&#39;Arcy Wentworth Thompson">D'Arcy Wentworth Thompson</a> (1860–1948) attempted to revive it.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Ruse&#95;22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ruse-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The psychiatrist and engineer <a href="/wiki/William_Ross_Ashby" class="mw-redirect" title="William Ross Ashby">W. Ross Ashby</a> introduced the term "self-organizing" to contemporary science in 1947.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ashby1947&#95;8-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ashby1947-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It was taken up by the cyberneticians <a href="/wiki/Heinz_von_Foerster" title="Heinz von Foerster">Heinz von Foerster</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gordon_Pask" title="Gordon Pask">Gordon Pask</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Stafford_Beer" class="mw-redirect" title="Anthony Stafford Beer">Stafford Beer</a>; and von Foerster organized a conference on "The Principles of Self-Organization" at the University of Illinois' Allerton Park in June, 1960 which led to a series of conferences on Self-Organizing Systems.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Norbert_Wiener" title="Norbert Wiener">Norbert Wiener</a> took up the idea in the second edition of his <i>Cybernetics: or Control and Communication in the Animal and the Machine</i> (1961).
</p><p>Self-organization was associated<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (May 2018)">by whom?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> with <a href="/wiki/Systems_theory" title="Systems theory">general systems theory</a> in the 1960s, but did not become commonplace in the scientific literature until physicists <a href="/wiki/Synergetics_(Haken)" title="Synergetics (Haken)">Hermann Haken</a> et al. and <a href="/wiki/Complex_system" title="Complex system">complex systems</a> researchers adopted it in a greater picture from cosmology <a href="/wiki/Erich_Jantsch#The_Self-Organizing_Universe,_1979" title="Erich Jantsch">Erich Jantsch</a>,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (May 2018)">clarification needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> chemistry with <a href="/wiki/Dissipative_system" title="Dissipative system">dissipative system</a>, biology and sociology as <a href="/wiki/Autopoiesis" title="Autopoiesis">autopoiesis</a> to <a href="/wiki/System_thinking" class="mw-redirect" title="System thinking">system thinking</a> in the following 1980s (<a href="/wiki/Santa_Fe_Institute" title="Santa Fe Institute">Santa Fe Institute</a>) and 1990s (<a href="/wiki/Complex_adaptive_system" title="Complex adaptive system">complex adaptive system</a>), until our days with the disruptive <a href="/wiki/Emerging_technologies" title="Emerging technologies">emerging technologies</a> profounded by a <a href="/wiki/Rhizome_(philosophy)" title="Rhizome (philosophy)">rhizomatic</a> <a href="/wiki/Network_theory" title="Network theory">network theory</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research" title="Wikipedia:No original research"><span title="The material near this tag possibly contains original research. (January 2022)">original research?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Around 2008–2009, a concept of guided self-organization started to take shape. This approach aims to regulate self-organization for specific purposes, so that a <a href="/wiki/Dynamical_system" title="Dynamical system">dynamical system</a> may reach specific attractors or outcomes. The regulation constrains a self-organizing process within a <a href="/wiki/Complex_system" title="Complex system">complex system</a> by restricting local interactions between the system components, rather than following an explicit control mechanism or a global design blueprint.  The desired outcomes, such as increases in the resultant internal structure and/or functionality, are achieved by combining task-independent global objectives with task-dependent constraints on local interactions.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="By_field">By field</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Self-organization&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: By field"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:ConvectionCells.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/ConvectionCells.svg/250px-ConvectionCells.svg.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="180" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/ConvectionCells.svg/500px-ConvectionCells.svg.png 1.5x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="360" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Convection_cell" title="Convection cell">Convection cells</a> in a gravity field</figcaption></figure>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Physics">Physics</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Self-organization&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Physics"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Self-assembly" title="Self-assembly">Self-assembly</a> and <a href="/wiki/Self-assembly_of_nanoparticles" title="Self-assembly of nanoparticles">Self-assembly of nanoparticles</a></div>
<p>The many self-organizing phenomena in <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">physics</a> include <a href="/wiki/Phase_transition" title="Phase transition">phase transitions</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spontaneous_symmetry_breaking" title="Spontaneous symmetry breaking">spontaneous symmetry breaking</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Spontaneous_magnetization" title="Spontaneous magnetization">spontaneous magnetization</a> and <a href="/wiki/Crystal_growth" title="Crystal growth">crystal growth</a> in <a href="/wiki/Classical_physics" title="Classical physics">classical physics</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Laser" title="Laser">laser</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Superconductivity" title="Superconductivity">superconductivity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bose%E2%80%93Einstein_condensation" class="mw-redirect" title="Bose–Einstein condensation">Bose–Einstein condensation</a> in <a href="/wiki/Quantum_physics" class="mw-redirect" title="Quantum physics">quantum physics</a>. Self-organization is found in <a href="/wiki/Self-organized_criticality" title="Self-organized criticality">self-organized criticality</a> in <a href="/wiki/Dynamical_system" title="Dynamical system">dynamical systems</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Tribology" title="Tribology">tribology</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Spin_foam" title="Spin foam">spin foam</a> systems, and in <a href="/wiki/Loop_quantum_gravity" title="Loop quantum gravity">loop quantum gravity</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> 
in <a href="/wiki/Plasma_(physics)" title="Plasma (physics)"> plasma</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
in river basins and deltas, in dendritic solidification (snow flakes), in capillary <a href="/wiki/Imbibition" title="Imbibition">imbibition</a><sup id="cite&#95;ref-YasugaIseri2021&#95;30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-YasugaIseri2021-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and in turbulent structure.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-:1&#95;5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Chemistry">Chemistry</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Self-organization&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Chemistry"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:DNA_nanostructures.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/DNA_nanostructures.png/250px-DNA_nanostructures.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="138" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/DNA_nanostructures.png/500px-DNA_nanostructures.png 1.5x" data-file-width="1260" data-file-height="693" /></a><figcaption>The DNA structure shown schematically at left self-assembles into the structure at right<sup id="cite&#95;ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></figcaption></figure>
<p>Self-organization in <a href="/wiki/Chemistry" title="Chemistry">chemistry</a> includes drying-induced self-assembly,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Molecular_self-assembly" title="Molecular self-assembly">molecular self-assembly</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Reaction%E2%80%93diffusion" class="mw-redirect" title="Reaction–diffusion">reaction–diffusion</a> systems and <a href="/wiki/Oscillating_reaction" class="mw-redirect" title="Oscillating reaction">oscillating reactions</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Autocatalysis" title="Autocatalysis">autocatalytic</a> networks, <a href="/wiki/Liquid_crystal" title="Liquid crystal">liquid crystals</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Grid_complex" title="Grid complex">grid complexes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Colloidal_crystals" class="mw-redirect" title="Colloidal crystals">colloidal crystals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Self-assembled_monolayer" title="Self-assembled monolayer">self-assembled monolayers</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>35<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Raval2003&#95;37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Raval2003-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Micelle" title="Micelle">micelles</a>, microphase separation of block <a href="/wiki/Copolymer" title="Copolymer">copolymers</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Langmuir%E2%80%93Blodgett_film" title="Langmuir–Blodgett film">Langmuir–Blodgett films</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><span class="anchor" id="Biology"></span>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Biology">Biology</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Self-organization&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Biology"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Biological_organization" class="mw-redirect" title="Biological organization">Biological organization</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Sort_sol_ved_%C3%98rns%C3%B8_2007.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Sort_sol_ved_%C3%98rns%C3%B8_2007.jpg/250px-Sort_sol_ved_%C3%98rns%C3%B8_2007.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="188" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Sort_sol_ved_%C3%98rns%C3%B8_2007.jpg/500px-Sort_sol_ved_%C3%98rns%C3%B8_2007.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1536" /></a><figcaption>Birds <a href="/wiki/Swarm_behavior" class="mw-redirect" title="Swarm behavior">flocking</a> (boids in Blender), an example of self-organization in biology</figcaption></figure>
<p>Self-organization in <a href="/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">biology</a><sup id="cite&#95;ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> can be observed in spontaneous <a href="/wiki/Protein_folding" title="Protein folding">folding of proteins</a> and other biomacromolecules, <a href="/wiki/Self-assembly" title="Self-assembly">self-assembly</a> of <a href="/wiki/Lipid_bilayer" title="Lipid bilayer">lipid bilayer</a> membranes, <a href="/wiki/Pattern_formation" title="Pattern formation">pattern formation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Morphogenesis" title="Morphogenesis">morphogenesis</a> in <a href="/wiki/Developmental_biology" title="Developmental biology">developmental biology</a>, the coordination of human movement, <a href="/wiki/Eusociality" title="Eusociality">eusocial behavior</a> in <a href="/wiki/Insect" title="Insect">insects</a> (<a href="/wiki/Bee" title="Bee">bees</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ant" title="Ant">ants</a>, <a href="/wiki/Termite" title="Termite">termites</a>)<sup id="cite&#95;ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Swarm_behavior" class="mw-redirect" title="Swarm behavior">flocking</a> behavior in birds and fish.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The mathematical biologist <a href="/wiki/Stuart_Kauffman" title="Stuart Kauffman">Stuart Kauffman</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Structuralism_(biology)" title="Structuralism (biology)">structuralists</a> have suggested that self-organization may play roles alongside <a href="/wiki/Natural_selection" title="Natural selection">natural selection</a> in three areas of <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_biology" title="Evolutionary biology">evolutionary biology</a>, namely <a href="/wiki/Population_dynamics" title="Population dynamics">population dynamics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Molecular_evolution" title="Molecular evolution">molecular evolution</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Morphogenesis" title="Morphogenesis">morphogenesis</a>. However, this does not take into account the essential role of <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a> in driving biochemical reactions in cells. The systems of reactions in any cell are <a href="/wiki/Catalysis" title="Catalysis">self-catalyzing</a>, but not simply self-organizing, as they are <a href="/wiki/Open_system_(thermodynamics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Open system (thermodynamics)">thermodynamically open systems</a> relying on a continuous input of energy.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Self-organization is not an alternative to natural selection, but it constrains what evolution can do and provides mechanisms such as the self-assembly of membranes which evolution then exploits.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The evolution of order in living systems and the generation of order in certain non-living systems was proposed to obey a common fundamental principal called “the Darwinian dynamic”<sup id="cite&#95;ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> that was formulated by first considering how microscopic order is generated in simple non-biological systems that are far from <a href="/wiki/Thermodynamic_equilibrium" title="Thermodynamic equilibrium">thermodynamic equilibrium</a>.  Consideration was then extended to short, replicating <a href="/wiki/RNA" title="RNA">RNA</a> molecules assumed to be similar to the earliest forms of life in the <a href="/wiki/RNA_world" title="RNA world">RNA world</a>.  It was shown that the underlying order-generating processes of self-organization in the non-biological systems and in replicating RNA are basically similar.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Cosmology">Cosmology</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Self-organization&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Cosmology"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>In his 1995 conference paper "Cosmology as a problem in critical phenomena" <a href="/wiki/Lee_Smolin" title="Lee Smolin">Lee Smolin</a> said that several cosmological objects or phenomena, such as <a href="/wiki/Spiral_galaxies" class="mw-redirect" title="Spiral galaxies">spiral galaxies</a>, <a href="/wiki/Galaxy_formation" class="mw-redirect" title="Galaxy formation">galaxy formation</a> processes in general, <a href="/wiki/Structure_formation" title="Structure formation">early structure formation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Quantum_gravity" title="Quantum gravity">quantum gravity</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Large_scale_structure_of_the_universe" class="mw-redirect" title="Large scale structure of the universe">large scale structure of the universe</a> might be the result of or have involved certain degree of self-organization.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>45<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> He argues that self-organized systems are often <a href="/wiki/Critical_system" title="Critical system">critical systems</a>, with structure spreading out in space and time over every available scale, as shown for example by <a href="/wiki/Per_Bak" title="Per Bak">Per Bak</a> and his collaborators. Therefore, because the <a href="/wiki/Distribution_of_matter_in_the_universe" class="mw-redirect" title="Distribution of matter in the universe">distribution of matter in the universe</a> is more or less scale invariant over many orders of magnitude, ideas and strategies developed in the study of self-organized systems could be helpful in tackling certain <a href="/wiki/Unsolved_problems_in_astronomy" class="mw-redirect" title="Unsolved problems in astronomy">unsolved problems in cosmology and astrophysics</a>.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Computer_science">Computer science</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Self-organization&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Computer science"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Phenomena from <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Computer_science" title="Computer science">computer science</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Cellular_automaton" title="Cellular automaton">cellular automata</a>, <a href="/wiki/Random_graph" title="Random graph">random graphs</a>, and some instances of <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_computation" title="Evolutionary computation">evolutionary computation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Artificial_life" title="Artificial life">artificial life</a> exhibit features of self-organization. In <a href="/wiki/Swarm_robotics" title="Swarm robotics">swarm robotics</a>, self-organization is used to produce emergent behavior. In particular the theory of random graphs has been used as a justification for self-organization as a general principle of complex systems. In the field of <a href="/wiki/Multi-agent_systems" class="mw-redirect" title="Multi-agent systems">multi-agent systems</a>, understanding how to engineer systems that are capable of presenting self-organized behavior is an active research area.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Optimization_algorithm" class="mw-redirect" title="Optimization algorithm">Optimization algorithms</a> can be considered self-organizing because they aim to find the optimal solution to a problem. If the solution is considered as a state of the iterative system, the optimal solution is the selected, converged structure of the system.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>48<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Self-organizing_networks" class="mw-redirect" title="Self-organizing networks">Self-organizing networks</a> include <a href="/wiki/Small-world_network" title="Small-world network">small-world networks</a><sup id="cite&#95;ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Self-stabilization" title="Self-stabilization">self-stabilization</a><sup id="cite&#95;ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>50<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Scale-free_network" title="Scale-free network">scale-free networks</a>. These emerge from bottom-up interactions, unlike top-down hierarchical networks within organizations, which are not self-organizing.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Clauset&#95;52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clauset-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>51<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Cloud computing systems have been argued to be inherently self-organizing,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> but while they have some autonomy, they are not self-managing as they do not have the goal of reducing their own complexity.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>53<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>54<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Cybernetics">Cybernetics</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Self-organization&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Cybernetics"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Self-organization_in_cybernetics" title="Self-organization in cybernetics">Self-organization in cybernetics</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Norbert_Wiener" title="Norbert Wiener">Norbert Wiener</a> regarded the automatic serial <a href="/wiki/System_identification" title="System identification">identification</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Black_box" title="Black box">black box</a> and its subsequent reproduction as self-organization in <a href="/wiki/Cybernetics" title="Cybernetics">cybernetics</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>55<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The importance of <a href="/wiki/Phase_locking" class="mw-redirect" title="Phase locking">phase locking</a> or the "attraction of frequencies", as he called it, is discussed in the 2nd edition of his <i><a href="/wiki/Cybernetics:_Or_Control_and_Communication_in_the_Animal_and_the_Machine" title="Cybernetics: Or Control and Communication in the Animal and the Machine">Cybernetics: Or Control and Communication in the Animal and the Machine</a></i>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>56<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/K._Eric_Drexler" title="K. Eric Drexler">K. Eric Drexler</a> sees <a href="/wiki/Molecular_assembler" title="Molecular assembler">self-replication</a> as a key step in nano and <a href="/wiki/Universal_assembler" class="mw-redirect" title="Universal assembler">universal assembly</a>. By contrast, the four concurrently connected galvanometers of <a href="/wiki/W._Ross_Ashby" title="W. Ross Ashby">W. Ross Ashby</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Homeostat" title="Homeostat">Homeostat</a> <a href="/wiki/Hunting_oscillation" title="Hunting oscillation">hunt</a>, when perturbed, to converge on one of many possible stable states.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>57<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Ashby used his state counting measure of <a href="/wiki/Variety_(cybernetics)" title="Variety (cybernetics)">variety</a><sup id="cite&#95;ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>58<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> to describe stable states and produced the "<a href="/wiki/Good_Regulator" class="mw-redirect" title="Good Regulator">Good Regulator</a>"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>59<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> theorem which requires internal models for self-organized <a href="/wiki/Endurantism" title="Endurantism">endurance</a> and stability (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Nyquist_stability_criterion" title="Nyquist stability criterion">Nyquist stability criterion</a>). <a href="/wiki/Warren_McCulloch" class="mw-redirect" title="Warren McCulloch">Warren McCulloch</a> proposed "Redundancy of Potential Command"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>60<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> as characteristic of the organization of the brain and human nervous system and the necessary condition for self-organization. <a href="/wiki/Heinz_von_Foerster" title="Heinz von Foerster">Heinz von Foerster</a> proposed Redundancy, <i>R</i>=1&#160;−&#160;<i>H</i>/<i>H</i><sub>max</sub>, where <i>H</i> is <a href="/wiki/Entropy" title="Entropy">entropy</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>61<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>62<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In essence this states that unused potential communication bandwidth is a measure of self-organization.
</p><p>In the 1970s <a href="/wiki/Stafford_Beer" title="Stafford Beer">Stafford Beer</a> considered self-organization necessary for <a href="/wiki/Autonomy" title="Autonomy">autonomy</a> in persisting and living systems. He applied his <a href="/wiki/Viable_system_model" title="Viable system model">viable system model</a> to management. It consists of five parts: the monitoring of performance of the survival processes (1), their management by recursive application of regulation (2), <a href="/wiki/Homeostasis" title="Homeostasis">homeostatic</a> operational control (3) and development (4) which produce maintenance of identity (5) under environmental perturbation. Focus is prioritized by an alerting "algedonic loop" feedback: a sensitivity to both pain and pleasure produced from under-performance or over-performance relative to a standard capability.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>63<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In the 1990s <a href="/wiki/Gordon_Pask" title="Gordon Pask">Gordon Pask</a> argued that von Foerster's H and Hmax were not independent, but <a href="/wiki/Gordon_Pask#Interactions_of_Actors_Theory" title="Gordon Pask">interacted</a> via <a href="/wiki/Countable_set" title="Countable set">countably infinite</a> recursive concurrent <a href="/wiki/Spin_(physics)" title="Spin (physics)">spin</a> processes<sup id="cite&#95;ref-p1996&#95;65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-p1996-65"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>64<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> which he called concepts. His strict definition of concept "a procedure to bring about a relation"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-p1973&#95;66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-p1973-66"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>65<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> permitted his theorem "Like concepts repel, unlike concepts attract"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>66<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> to state a general spin-based principle of self-organization. His edict, an exclusion principle, "There are <a href="/wiki/Gordon_Pask#No_Doppelgangers" title="Gordon Pask">No Doppelgangers</a>" means no two concepts can be the same. After sufficient time, all concepts attract and coalesce as <a href="/wiki/Pink_noise" title="Pink noise">pink noise</a>. The theory applies to all organizationally <a href="/wiki/Closure_(topology)" title="Closure (topology)">closed</a> or homeostatic processes that produce <a href="/wiki/Endurantism" title="Endurantism">enduring</a> and <a href="/wiki/Coherence_(physics)" title="Coherence (physics)">coherent</a> products which evolve, learn and adapt.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>67<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-p1996&#95;65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-p1996-65"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>64<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Sociology">Sociology</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Self-organization&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Sociology"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Spontaneous_order" title="Spontaneous order">Spontaneous order</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:CIA_Map_of_International_illegal_drug_connections.gif" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/CIA_Map_of_International_illegal_drug_connections.gif/250px-CIA_Map_of_International_illegal_drug_connections.gif" decoding="async" width="250" height="134" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/CIA_Map_of_International_illegal_drug_connections.gif/450px-CIA_Map_of_International_illegal_drug_connections.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/ef/CIA_Map_of_International_illegal_drug_connections.gif 2x" data-file-width="450" data-file-height="241" /></a><figcaption>Social self-organization in international drug routes</figcaption></figure>
<p>The self-organizing behavior of social animals and the self-organization of simple mathematical structures both suggest that self-organization should be expected in human <a href="/wiki/Society" title="Society">society</a>. Tell-tale signs of self-organization are usually statistical properties shared with self-organizing physical systems. Examples such as <a href="/wiki/Critical_mass_(sociodynamics)" title="Critical mass (sociodynamics)">critical mass</a>, <a href="/wiki/Herd_behavior" title="Herd behavior">herd behavior</a>, <a href="/wiki/Groupthink" title="Groupthink">groupthink</a> and others, abound in <a href="/wiki/Sociology" title="Sociology">sociology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Economics" title="Economics">economics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Behavioral_finance" class="mw-redirect" title="Behavioral finance">behavioral finance</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anthropology" title="Anthropology">anthropology</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>68<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
<a href="/wiki/Spontaneous_order" title="Spontaneous order">Spontaneous order</a> can be influenced by <a href="/wiki/Arousal" title="Arousal">arousal</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>69<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In social theory, the concept of self-referentiality has been introduced as a sociological application of self-organization theory by <a href="/wiki/Niklas_Luhmann" title="Niklas Luhmann">Niklas Luhmann</a> (1984). For Luhmann the elements of a social system are self-producing communications, i.e. a communication produces further communications and hence a social system can reproduce itself as long as there is dynamic communication. For Luhmann, human beings are sensors in the environment of the system. Luhmann developed an evolutionary theory of society and its subsystems, using functional analyses and systems theory.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>70<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Anarchism" title="Anarchism">Anarchism</a> can advocate self-organization as one of its basic principles.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>71<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Economics">Economics</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Self-organization&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Economics"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Market_economy" title="Market economy">market economy</a> is sometimes said to be self-organizing. <a href="/wiki/Paul_Krugman" title="Paul Krugman">Paul Krugman</a> has written on the role that market self-organization plays in the business cycle in his book <i>The Self Organizing Economy</i>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>72<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Hayek" title="Friedrich Hayek">Friedrich Hayek</a> coined the term <i><a href="/wiki/Catallaxy" title="Catallaxy">catallaxy</a></i><sup id="cite&#95;ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>73<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> to describe a "self-organizing system of voluntary co-operation", in regards to the spontaneous order of the free market economy. <a href="/wiki/Neoclassical_economics" title="Neoclassical economics">Neo-classical economists</a> hold that imposing <a href="/wiki/Central_planning" class="mw-redirect" title="Central planning">central planning</a> usually makes the self-organized economic system less efficient. On the other end of the spectrum, economists consider that <a href="/wiki/Market_failure" title="Market failure">market failures</a> are so significant that self-organization produces bad results and that the state should direct production and pricing. Most economists adopt an intermediate position and recommend a mixture of market economy and <a href="/wiki/Command_economy" class="mw-redirect" title="Command economy">command economy</a> characteristics (sometimes called a <a href="/wiki/Mixed_economy" title="Mixed economy">mixed economy</a>). When applied to economics, the concept of self-organization can quickly become ideologically imbued.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Biel2009&#95;75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Biel2009-75"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>74<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>75<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Learning">Learning</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Self-organization&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Learning"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Enabling others to "learn how to learn"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>76<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> is often taken to mean instructing them<sup id="cite&#95;ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>77<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> how to submit to being taught. Self-organized learning (SOL)<sup id="cite&#95;ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>78<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>79<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>80<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> denies that "the expert knows best" or that there is ever "the one best method",<sup id="cite&#95;ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>81<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>82<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>83<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> insisting instead on "the construction of personally significant, relevant and viable meaning"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>84<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> to be tested experientially by the learner.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>85<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This may be collaborative, and more rewarding personally.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>86<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>87<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It is seen as a lifelong process, not limited to specific learning environments (home, school, university) or under the control of authorities such as parents and professors.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>88<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It needs to be tested, and intermittently revised, through the personal experience of the learner.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>89<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It need not be restricted by either consciousness or language.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>90<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Fritjof_Capra" title="Fritjof Capra">Fritjof Capra</a> argued that it is poorly recognized within psychology and education.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>91<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It may be related to cybernetics as it involves a <a href="/wiki/Negative_feedback" title="Negative feedback">negative feedback</a> control loop,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-p1973&#95;66-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-p1973-66"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>65<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> or to <a href="/wiki/Systems_theory" title="Systems theory">systems theory</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>92<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It can be conducted as a learning conversation or dialog between learners or within one person.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>93<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>94<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Transportation">Transportation</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Self-organization&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Transportation"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Three-phase_traffic_theory" title="Three-phase traffic theory">Three-phase traffic theory</a></div>
<p>The self-organizing behavior of drivers in <a href="/wiki/Traffic_flow" title="Traffic flow">traffic flow</a> determines almost all the spatiotemporal behavior of traffic, such as traffic breakdown at a highway bottleneck, highway capacity, and the emergence of moving traffic jams. These self-organizing effects are explained by <a href="/wiki/Boris_Kerner" title="Boris Kerner">Boris Kerner</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Three-phase_traffic_theory" title="Three-phase traffic theory">three-phase traffic theory</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>95<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Linguistics">Linguistics</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Self-organization&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Linguistics"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Order appears spontaneously in the <a href="/wiki/Linguistic_evolution" class="mw-redirect" title="Linguistic evolution">evolution of language</a> as individual and population behavior interacts with biological evolution.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>96<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Research">Research</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Self-organization&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Research"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p><b>Self-organized funding allocation</b> (<b>SOFA</b>) is a method of distributing <a href="/wiki/Funding_of_science" title="Funding of science">funding</a> for scientific <a href="/wiki/Research" title="Research">research</a>. In this system, each researcher is allocated an equal amount of funding, and is required to anonymously allocate a fraction of their funds to the research of others. Proponents of SOFA argue that it would result in similar distribution of funding as the present grant system, but with less overhead.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>97<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In 2016, a test pilot of SOFA began in the Netherlands.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>98<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Criticism">Criticism</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Self-organization&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Criticism"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Heinz_Pagels" title="Heinz Pagels">Heinz Pagels</a>, in a 1985 review of <a href="/wiki/Ilya_Prigogine" title="Ilya Prigogine">Ilya Prigogine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Isabelle_Stengers" title="Isabelle Stengers">Isabelle Stengers</a>'s book <i>Order Out of Chaos</i> in <i><a href="/wiki/Physics_Today" title="Physics Today">Physics Today</a></i>, appeals to authority:<sup id="cite&#95;ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>99<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1244412712" /><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Most scientists would agree with the critical view expressed in <i>Problems of Biological Physics</i> (Springer Verlag, 1981) by the biophysicist L. A. Blumenfeld, when he wrote: "The meaningful macroscopic ordering of biological structure does not arise due to the increase of certain parameters of a system above their critical values. These structures are built according to program-like complicated architectural structures, the meaningful information created during many billions of years of chemical and biological evolution being used." Life is a consequence of microscopic, not macroscopic, organization.</p></blockquote>
<p>Of course, Blumenfeld does not answer the further question of how those program-like structures emerge in the first place. His explanation leads directly to <a href="/wiki/Infinite_regress" title="Infinite regress">infinite regress</a>.
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1244412712" /><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>In short, they [Prigogine and Stengers] maintain that <a href="/wiki/Arrow_of_time" title="Arrow of time">time irreversibility</a> is not derived from a time-independent microworld, but is itself fundamental. The virtue of their idea is that it resolves what they perceive as a "clash of doctrines" about the nature of <a href="/wiki/Time_in_physics" title="Time in physics">time in physics</a>. Most physicists would agree that there is neither empirical evidence to support their view, nor is there a mathematical necessity for it. There is no "clash of doctrines." Only Prigogine and a few colleagues hold to these speculations which, in spite of their efforts, continue to live in the twilight zone of scientific credibility.</p></blockquote>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Theology" title="Theology">theology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Aquinas" title="Thomas Aquinas">Thomas Aquinas</a> (1225–1274) in his <i><a href="/wiki/Summa_Theologica" title="Summa Theologica">Summa Theologica</a></i> assumes a <a href="/wiki/Teleology" title="Teleology">teleological</a> created universe in rejecting the idea that something can be a self-sufficient cause of its own organization:<sup id="cite&#95;ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>100<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1244412712" /><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Since nature works for a determinate end under the direction of a higher agent, whatever is done by nature must needs be traced back to God, as to its first cause. So also whatever is done voluntarily must also be traced back to some higher cause other than human reason or will, since these can change or fail; for all things that are changeable and capable of defect must be traced back to an immovable and self-necessary first principle, as was shown in the body of the Article.</p></blockquote>

<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Notes">Notes</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Self-organization&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Notes"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>

<li id="cite&#95;note-24"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-24">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">
As an indication of the increasing importance of this concept, when queried with the keyword <code>self-organ*</code>, <i>Dissertation Abstracts</i> finds nothing before 1954, and only four entries before 1970. There were 17 in the years 1971–1980; 126 in 1981–1990; and 593 in 1991–2000.</span>
